  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_37876024-2cec-4add-8675-d5216f8bb2a4'

  <span id="freeText12625293634559216510" style="display:none">This is the way the world ends... for the last time. <br /><br />The Moon will soon return. Whether this heralds the destruction of humankind or something worse will depend on two women.<br /><br />Essun has inherited the power of Alabaster Tenring. With it, she hopes to find her daughter Nassun and forge a world in which every orogene child can grow up safe.<br /><br />For Nassun, her mother's mastery of the Obelisk Gate comes too late. She has seen the evil of the world, and accepted what her mother will not admit: that sometimes what is corrupt cannot be cleansed, only destroyed.<br /><br /><b>The remarkable conclusion to the post-apocalyptic and highly acclaimed trilogy that began with the multi-award-nominated <i>The Fifth Season.</i></b></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2068625584" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068625584" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3030788-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577914102p2/3030788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2068625584?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/3030788-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=own-voices">own-voices</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=buddy-reads">buddy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3030788-melanie?shelf=lgbtqiap">lgbtqiap</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2068625584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer844932139105171196"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1501547414i/23469117._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><i>ARC provided by Hachette in exchange for an honest review.</i><br/><br/>1.) <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567118098' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Fifth Season</a> ★★★★★<br/>2.) <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2063711531' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Obelisk Gate</a> ★★★★<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Some worlds are built on a fault line of pain, held up by nightmares. Don't lament when those worlds fall. Rage that they were built doomed in the first place.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/>You guys, I’m speechless. I’m not sure if I’ve ever read as perfect of a conclusion as <i>The Stone Sky</i>. <i>The Stone Sky</i> easily makes my best of 2017 list, and is also without a doubt one of the most powerful masterpieces I</span>
  <span id="freeText844932139105171196" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1501547414i/23469117._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>ARC provided by Hachette in exchange for an honest review.</i><br><br>1.) <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567118098" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a> ★★★★★<br>2.) <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2063711531" rel="nofollow noopener">The Obelisk Gate</a> ★★★★<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Some worlds are built on a fault line of pain, held up by nightmares. Don't lament when those worlds fall. Rage that they were built doomed in the first place.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>You guys, I’m speechless. I’m not sure if I’ve ever read as perfect of a conclusion as <i>The Stone Sky</i>. <i>The Stone Sky</i> easily makes my best of 2017 list, and is also without a doubt one of the most powerful masterpieces I’ve ever read in my entire life. I will cherish this book series until the end of my days, while also trying to convince every single living soul to give this series a shot. Please give <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567118098" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a> a shot. It is worth more than every ounce of hype and praise it has received. I recommend this series to any and everyone I know. Not just SFF lovers, hell, not even just book lovers; I recommend this to every human being. And I dare you to finish this series, turn that last page, and not feel the urge to change this ugly world we live in today.<br><br>This series is a SFF dystopian, where earthquakes, volcanic eruptions, and other terrible things impacting the earth are constantly happening, but orogenes are able to manipulate the earth to ease them. Even though orogenes are continually saving the world they are constantly oppressed slaves. This world has convinced everyone that orogenes are dangerous and need to be controlled at all costs. Everyone in the Stillness is trying to survive the world's unforgiving environment. This planet is beyond unstable, because of Fifth Seasons. Two years have passed since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567118098" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a> and in this concluding book our main characters are looking for a way to stop the Seasons once and for all.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“They’re afraid because we exist, she says, There’s nothing we did to provoke their fear, other than exist. There’s nothing we can do to earn their approval, except stop existing—so we can either die like they want, or laugh at their cowardice and go on with our lives.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>The greatest thing about this series is that it seamlessly mirrors the world we live in today. This book will make you think about your internalized racism and the prejudices that you hold without even realizing it. I mean, look at what is going on in the United States right now. Look at how we are allowing actual Nazis <s>free</s> hate speech. Look who we elected, because people’s hearts were filled with so much hate. Look how we are trying to protect confederate statues, while allowing our government to bulldoze native sites for pipelines. Hate is a powerful force, and white supremacy is real. Charlottesville is happening all over our world, and we don’t need orogeny to stop it, either.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1503296359i/23646579._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“But for a society built on exploitation, there is no greater threat than having no one left to oppress.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>I wrote in my review for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2063711531" rel="nofollow noopener">The Obelisk Gate</a> that the heart of this novel is oppression, but the soul of this novel is motherhood, and I stand by this assessment even more so. Again, I’m not a mother, but the underlying theme of parenthood and the indescribable love between a mother and child is something so pure and beautiful. I can’t even begin to describe the feelings and emotions this book was able to evoke from me. <br><br>The constant messages and reminders of the importance of found families is also something that I appreciate with every bone in my body. I don’t want to keep using the word beautiful, but these messages that N.K. Jemisin has created are nothing short of the word beautiful. Blood is just that, blood, but choosing to spend your days with people who unconditionally love and support you is the true meaning of family.<br><br>Just thinking of the people who have followed Essun throughout her journey makes me weep from equal parts of joy and sadness. I loved seeing people love the broken parts of Essun, seeing her friends love the strong woman she always was all along, seeing her family choose to follow her to the end of the Earth.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“…if you love someone, you don’t get to choose how they love you back.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>And seeing the choices that Nassun made all by herself from both places of hurt and love just broke my heart. The choices we all make from being hurt or being loved is a discussion I could write pages and pages on. The feelings and emotions in this book are so very complex and the narrative only makes you feel everything intensified. Right now, as I’m writing this review, I’m equal parts heartbreak and hope.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“It’s just that love and hate aren’t mutually exclusive”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br>And the representation in this book is the best I’ve ever read in all my years. First off, this book is unapologetically and beautifully black. Next, N.K. Jemisin writes about systematic oppression expertly. Then, she also seamlessly writes in LGBT+ representation effortlessly. This book has the best written trans side character I’ve ever read about. I’ve said it before, and I’ll scream it from the rooftops again: every author should strive to write representation like N.K. Jemisin. <br><br>The writing is also exquisite. The prose is a tier above the rest. The narrative in unique and heartfelt. The world building is nothing short of perfection. The themes are relevant, important, and inspiring. The acknowledgments broke my heart. This series is truly a masterpiece. <br><br>This is one of the best stories I’ve ever read in my entire life. No amount of words I can write here is going to do it justice, so I can only ask, or beg, you to pick it up and see for yourself. Thank you, N.K. Jemisin, for this masterpiece. I will never stop moving forward, and I will never stop fighting for a better world. <br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“We could’ve all been safe and comfortable together, surviving together, but they didn’t want that. Now nobody gets to be safe. Maybe that’s what it will take for them to finally realize things have to change.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><a href="https://meltotheany.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.twitter.com/meltotheany" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://meltotheany.tumblr.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/meltotheany/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/channel/UC4RbHEg_fH6CewJrh_0xJ9g" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a href="http://www.twitch.tv/meltotheany" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitch</a> <br><br>Buddy Read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974290179?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Mary</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793621327" rel="nofollow noopener">Petrik</a> ❤</span>
  <a data-text-id="844932139105171196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1793621327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1793621327" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793621327?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316229241" href="/book/show/33296259-the-stone-sky">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Mix of high fantasy &amp; Sci fi fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59458347-petrik?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793621327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18139417352676755997"><i>Buddy read with my favorite Orogenes: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068625584?book_show_action=false' rel='nofollow noopener'>Melanie</a> & <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974290179?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mary</a></i><br/><br/><b>Jemisin has truly outdone herself with this book and trilogy. The Stone Sky, contrary to my expectation, has somehow become one of the best conclusions to a trilogy I’ve ever read, it’s simply extraordinary.</b><br/><br/>My experience reading this trilogy can be summed up as if I’m on a see-saw. I loved the first book, dislike and disappointed with the second book, and then this one, I absolutely loved it. I was never bored with it and loved every single mom</span>
  <span id="freeText18139417352676755997" style="display:none"><i>Buddy read with my favorite Orogenes: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2068625584?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">Melanie</a> &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974290179?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Mary</a></i><br /><br /><b>Jemisin has truly outdone herself with this book and trilogy. The Stone Sky, contrary to my expectation, has somehow become one of the best conclusions to a trilogy I’ve ever read, it’s simply extraordinary.</b><br /><br />My experience reading this trilogy can be summed up as if I’m on a see-saw. I loved the first book, dislike and disappointed with the second book, and then this one, I absolutely loved it. I was never bored with it and loved every single moment reading this book. I’ll be honest here, somewhere around the middle of this book, I thought to myself <i>
  <b>“Hmm okay this is great but it seems like Jemisin can’t surpass what she did in The Fifth Season”</b>
</i>, I couldn’t be more wrong. <br /><br />If I was to judge this trilogy only from the first book, I would never have thought that the scope of the story will ever become this gigantic. I mean, the story spanned for thousands of years, and I love how it reminds us that the past will always influence the future, but it doesn’t mean we have to live and be stuck in it.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“How can we prepare for the future if we won’t acknowledge the past?”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />Every question you have on the story so far will be answered here, what the Obelisk Gate truly is, the origin of Orogene, how the Shattering occurred, what caused the endless Fifth Season, everything and I mean literally everything from the first book is a preparation for the last five chapters of this book, which was full of revelations and imbued with emotionally thrilling climax sequences. <b>It was without a doubt groundbreakingly marvelous.</b><br /><br />I have to also note that the world-building and prose in this installment are insanely good. Anybody who’s a fan of great world-building will definitely love this book and overall trilogy. However, the best part about The Stone Sky imo is its stellar character developments and interactions. As Jemisin said, the main theme of the trilogy is not about the post-apocalyptic world, the science, nope, they’re all great but the main theme at its core is about love and motherhood. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“I think,” Hoa says slowly, “that if you love someone, you don’t get to choose how they love you back.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />I can't stress this highly enough, <b>this trilogy contains one of the finest storytelling on the topic of motherhood and parenthood in a sci-fi fantasy story I’ve ever read, probably ever.</b> It tells us once again just how important love is, not even in a romance kind of way but just loves, towards your friends, family, and even strangers. Because this book is wonderful in its way of telling us how not only someone blood related, but a stranger could also become someone you can consider family within a short period of time.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“She has seen him fight his own brutal nature, and the Earth itself, in order to be the parent she needs. He has helped her learn to love herself for what she is.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br />I haven’t read any of Jemisin’s other series but I highly doubt that she ever wrote something as great as this installment. I don’t even know how she’ll be able to surpass this particular book in the future. <b>The Stone Sky is truly a stunning conclusion to a trilogy, I didn’t expect to love this one as much as I do, but this is definitely going on my 'favorites' shelf.</b> It’s beautiful, poignant, and most of all, emotionally impactful. This is due to the reason that Jemisin faced truly hard moments during the time of publishing this book, specifically on her mother’s passing. <br /><br />You can read it in detail in the acknowledgment section, and you MUST do so. I legit almost cried reading the acknowledgment, I’m closing this review with a small section from it, and what I'm sure Jemisin is trying to convey to us all on what 'The Stone Sky' means to her and her readers. Even with my personal dislike about the second book, <b><i>The Broken Earth</i> trilogy is still a journey worth undertaking, Jemisin has poured all her emotions into this book and you won’t regret reading it.</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“I definitely haven’t been in the best place while working on this book, but I can say this much: Where there is pain in this book, it is real pain; where there is anger, it is real anger; where there is love, it is real love. You’ve been taking this journey with me, and you’re always going to get the best of what I’ve got. That’s what my mother would want.” – N. K. Jemisin</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <u>Series review</u>
</b><br /><br />The Fifth Season: <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1783986134?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">4.5/5 Stars</a>
</u><br /><br />The Obelisk Gate: <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1783986608?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">2.5/5 Stars</a>
</u><br /><br />The Stone Sky: <b>
  <u>5/5 Stars</u>
</b><br /><br />The Broken Earth trilogy: <b>
  <u>12/15 Stars</u>
</b><br /><br /><i>You can find <a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/26-pat-reviews/739-thestonesky" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a> and the rest of my Adult Epic/High Fantasy &amp; Sci-Fi reviews at <a href="http://booknest.eu/review/patrick" rel="nofollow noopener">BookNest</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="18139417352676755997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15631728963809330170">There's really no easy way to put this, so I'll come right out and say it.<br/><br/>This is one of the very best stories I've ever read. <br/><br/>All together now, all three books in this trilogy, together, make up one hell of a great story.<br/><br/>I am amazed. I cried. I was blown away by the sheer immensity of what was going on, of the implications and the revelations and the final action.<br/><br/>Sure, we knew that one of two things must happen by the end of the second book, but I hadn't quite realized just how invested I'd hav</span>
  <span id="freeText15631728963809330170" style="display:none">There's really no easy way to put this, so I'll come right out and say it.<br /><br />This is one of the very best stories I've ever read. <br /><br />All together now, all three books in this trilogy, together, make up one hell of a great story.<br /><br />I am amazed. I cried. I was blown away by the sheer immensity of what was going on, of the implications and the revelations and the final action.<br /><br />Sure, we knew that one of two things must happen by the end of the second book, but I hadn't quite realized just how invested I'd have gotten by that point. I didn't know how it would happen or what kinds of complications might arise or just how much enemies had turned into allies or who was good or bad... because that was never the point of these books.<br /><br />We are all people. Every single one of us... whether stone eater, rogga, or still. The fact that the point is far from belabored, rather gorgeous in exploration and execution, makes it more than icing on this cake. I'm simply shaken to my core.<br /><br />This is one of the best stories I've ever read.<br /><br />It's more than sheer imagination, storytelling skill, world-building, or fantastically complicated characters or world-shattering events. It's ART.<br /><br />I am 100% squealing fanboy here.<br /><br />I actually whooped aloud as I was reading and startled my daughter. :)<br /><br />THIS is why I read. This is the sheer fascination I always try to hold onto. :)<br /><br />THANK YOU!</span>

<div id="review_2401306865" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2401306865" />
    <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines"><img alt="Elle (ellexamines)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582250895p2/52545435.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2401306865?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elle (ellexamines)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elle (ellexamines)" href="/user/show/52545435-elle-ellexamines">Elle (ellexamines)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=z-read2018">z-read2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=y-out2017">y-out2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=x-series">x-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=x-audiobook">x-audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52545435-elle-ellexamines?shelf=sff-high-fantasy">sff-high-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2401306865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer459319897329736214"><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“I think,” Hoa says slowly, “that if you love someone, you don’t get to choose how they love you back.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/>About the same quality as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2397919326' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Obelisk Gate</a>, honestly, but with that STUNNING ending tacked on. <br/><br/>I’ve talked about so much with regards to this series. Oh my god. You can always check out my reviews of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1946460674' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Fifth Season</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2025939755' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Obelisk Gate</a>, but I’ll give a brief summary of my thoughts. It won two Hugo awards. The characters could kill me and I’d be okay with it. The worldbuilding is i</span>
  <span id="freeText459319897329736214" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“I think,” Hoa says slowly, “that if you love someone, you don’t get to choose how they love you back.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br />About the same quality as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2397919326" rel="nofollow noopener">The Obelisk Gate</a>, honestly, but with that STUNNING ending tacked on. <br /><br />I’ve talked about so much with regards to this series. Oh my god. You can always check out my reviews of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1946460674" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2025939755" rel="nofollow noopener">The Obelisk Gate</a>, but I’ll give a brief summary of my thoughts. It won two Hugo awards. The characters could kill me and I’d be okay with it. The worldbuilding is incredibly complex but not so convoluted as to be boring. The first book, which is still the best one, is a twisty mess and I adored it. There are reveals everywhere, about the world and the characters and the plot and so much more. The buildup and payoff dynamics are excellent. The thematic core around oppression is fucking awesome and done with an all-black and majority-queer cast.<br /><br />But let’s talk about this book, specifically.<br /><br /><br /><i>
  <b>→complaints and errors←</b>
</i><br /><br />✔I was really disappointed by the <b>lack of focus on side characters.</b> The side characters of books one and two were, in many ways, what made the series for me, and I am so fucking mad about how little we got out of them in this book. I mean, I love Hoa and Nassun, but I love all of them and you should let me live. <br /><br />✔I still <b>don’t love Essun</b> as much as I did in book one. She’s really dislikable in several areas that I’m not good at forgiving, and it just taints a lot of her for me, and I feel sort of awful about it.<br /> <br />✔I think I also… <b>didn’t understand the backstory chapters</b> very well, when I understood them at all. This is the first book of this series that I’ve read entirely on audiobook, and though I absolutely <i>adore</i> Robin Miles and want you all to see what a fantastic job she does narrating these books, I don’t think this particular book is necessarily the best choice for an audiobook. <br /><br /><br /><i>
  <b>→interlude: I share my opinions←</b>
</i><br />In this book, we again get povs from three characters; Essun, Nassun, and a new narrator - Hoa. <br /><br />➽<i>Essun</i> - This character is so mentally strong, so drily sarcastic, and so gorgeously developed. I have also finally noticed that she is very unlikable. I shockingly still empathize with her but I’m also mad at her and I would fight her if not for the fact that she definitely can and would kill me. And her second person still works for me.<br /><br />➽<i>Nassun</i> - Essun’s daughter is becoming more and more of an antihero over time. I love villain arcs, and her villain arc specifically is so great; I love how she’s forced to learn to be manipulative, to play the emotions of her father and so many others. She’s also still such a fucking… teenager, and I find it really endearing and deeply sympathetic and yeah. I really like her. She’s my favorite. Give her a girlfriend and also eternal happiness.<br /><br />➽<i>Hoa</i> - Let’s reveal some actual past to this world, finally. Via the biggest angel of an immortal deity I’ve ever had the pleasure of reading about. Thematic motif: all the characters in this series are just Closet Soft Boys. [Alabaster would fight me for saying this but I don’t care.]<br /><br /><br /><i>
  <b>→and now back to the good←</b>
</i><br />This series has just been… a really good experience for me. In general. And I love these characters, so much, and I don’t think I could ever really hate a book containing them. So, we have a new narrator who we’ve all been desperately awaiting for a book now, and the characters - especially Nassun - have changed a lot since book two. Which is so awesome. You can really feel the development in them and the conflict within them. <br /><br />But you know what really <i>made</i> this book for me? <b>The conflict around motherhood.</b><br /><br />I mean, since the beginning, this series has been adamant about how loving someone is not enough for them to know you love them, and drilled into our heads that Essun has not always been the best mother. But I wasn’t expecting… payoff. I was not expecting the degree to which a mother can mistreat her daughter, despite loving her, despite thinking she is doing the right thing, to become the focus. <br /><br />I <i>love</i> that it has become the focus.<br /><br />I can’t believe what a good job this story has done at balancing Nassun and Essun in this conflict, at seeing both their motivations and realizing that neither one is completely wrong or completely right, not really. <br /><br />And then… the ending. It’s just stunning. I love the writing of it - we finally see a powerplay we’ve been building up to for three books. I love the character bit of it - it is utterly gutwrenching. And I love the hope in it, and the sense that even though the world is fucked up, there is always, always hope. If you look hard enough. <br /><br /><a href="https://thebookishactress.wordpress.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/52545435" rel="nofollow noopener">Goodreads</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/jespergayhey" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://www.youtube.com/channel/UCVIY_GxZIuclzsIw9CLSe3g" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="459319897329736214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1974290179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974290179" />
    <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes"><img alt="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598297811p2/1452829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1974290179?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes">Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=year-2017">year-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1974290179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15907208839853722838"><b>
  <i>Oh well gee, golly, gosh, let me just to review this masterpiece, no problem, easy peasy???</i>
</b><br/><br/>The further I get into a series, the harder it is for me to review each individual book because <b>so much of my opinion</b> relies on understanding everything that happened in the preceding books. More than usual, I find it <u>so difficult</u> to express what this series has made me feel. <br/><br/>Just saying <i>"it was amazing"</i> or <i>"it was mind-blowing"</i> starts to feel repetitive & those common compliments don't really do the n</span>
  <span id="freeText15907208839853722838" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Oh well gee, golly, gosh, let me just to review this masterpiece, no problem, easy peasy???</i>
</b><br /><br />The further I get into a series, the harder it is for me to review each individual book because <b>so much of my opinion</b> relies on understanding everything that happened in the preceding books. More than usual, I find it <u>so difficult</u> to express what this series has made me feel. <br /><br />Just saying <i>"it was amazing"</i> or <i>"it was mind-blowing"</i> starts to feel repetitive &amp; those common compliments don't really do the novel justice. That is 1000% the case with <i>The Broken Earth</i> trilogy. <br /><br />This series is <b>so complex, so full of raw emotion, &amp; so beautifully distinct from other books of this genre.</b><br /><br />The writing here is some of the most engrossing I've ever read in my life. Jemisin is equal parts artist &amp; scientist with her eloquent prose &amp; meticulously organized plot points. <br /><br />I always say that I struggle to stay engaged with Hard Sci-Fi. I don't know if these books should be classified as Hard Sci-Fi, as they don't drone on &amp; on in bouts of irrelevant tech speak, but they're similar in that there are <b>so many facets to the science.</b><br /><br />But where Hard Sci-Fi often strikes me as boring, this series had me solidly engaged. It seems almost as though it's morphed together a handful sub-genres under the SFF banner &amp; created an umbrella all its own.<br /><br />The scope of this story makes me feel <i>so small;</i> it is so full of detail &amp; history that I feel as though I'm reading about events that have actually played out in some far away universe. <br /><br />Something I really appreciate in a series is <b>reread value.</b> I have no doubt <i>The Broken Earth</i> is a series that will have more to reveal upon each read through.<br /><br />The wrap of to this series is <b>devastating</b> but also <b>wonderfully appropriate &amp; well-crafted.</b> I believe with this concluding novel Jemisin has <b>truly set herself apart,</b> not just as an author, but also as a vital contributor to the direction in which Fantasy will evolve as time goes on. <br /><br />I can say no more than to <b>read this series</b> &amp; take the journey for yourself, but I will caution you that you <b>
  <u>must have patience.</u>
</b> Nothing is immediately clear, and this story takes its time revealing itself to you, but I promise it is <i>more than worth it.</i><br /><br />Honored to have read this with the wonderful, beautiful, amazing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/3030788-melanie" rel="nofollow noopener">Melanie</a> 💜 and the sweet, adorable, darling <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/59458347-petrik" rel="nofollow noopener">Petrik</a>! 💜<br /><br /><i><a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/34-mary-s-reviews/766-thestonesky" rel="nofollow noopener">This review</a> and other reviews of mine can be found on <a href="http://booknest.eu/" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Nest</a>!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15907208839853722838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2128509066" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2128509066" />
    <a title="Nick Imrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie"><img alt="Nick Imrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591436280p2/5906293.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2128509066?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick Imrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Imrie" href="/user/show/5906293-nick-imrie">Nick Imrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=fantasy-grimdark">fantasy-grimdark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=post-apocalypse">post-apocalypse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5906293-nick-imrie?shelf=award-hugo">award-hugo</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2128509066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15845339728116432619">This book opens at the peak of edgy grimdark with this little gem:<br/><blockquote>And I will tell you everything of how, later, as the quiet of death descended, I whispered:<br/>Right now.<br/><i>Right now.</i><br/>And the Earth whispered back:<br/><i>Burn.</i></blockquote><br/>Which is so melodramatic that I could only imagine Hoa dressed as a teenage goth girl and I laughed out loud.<br/><br/>The Broken Earth is hateful trilogy of hating; so it's appropriate that book 3 emphasised that the Earth was alive and conscious and really really hated humanity. Like every other </span>
  <span id="freeText15845339728116432619" style="display:none">This book opens at the peak of edgy grimdark with this little gem:<br /><blockquote>And I will tell you everything of how, later, as the quiet of death descended, I whispered:<br />Right now.<br /><i>Right now.</i><br />And the Earth whispered back:<br /><i>Burn.</i></blockquote><br />Which is so melodramatic that I could only imagine Hoa dressed as a teenage goth girl and I laughed out loud.<br /><br />The Broken Earth is hateful trilogy of hating; so it's appropriate that book 3 emphasised that the Earth was alive and conscious and really really hated humanity. Like every other character in this book, Father Earth was petty and resentful, even going so far as to descend to the most childish of self-justifications: <i>'You started this!'</i> He wanted revenge on humans because they tried to drill to the core, without stopping to think about how Earth would feel about that. Of course, humans didn't know that Earth was living and conscious, so they never gave a second though to his feelings. This was presented as a terrible failure on the part of humanity:<br /><br /><blockquote>So where they should have seen a living being; they saw only another thing to exploit. Where they should have asked, or left alone, they raped.</blockquote><br /><br />Well, I'm sorry, but Father Earth was actually the one at fault here. Humans didn't know that Earth was conscious, but Earth knew that humans were. Why didn't he make some attempt to reach out and communicate? Instead, he just bided his time, let humans continue in their blithe ignorance, and went straight to genocide as his first move. Not cool, Earth. Not cool.<br /><br />The next paragraph got weird and vindictive:<br /><br /><blockquote>For some crimes there is no fitting justice – only reparation. So for every iota of life siphoned from beneath the Earth's skin, the Earth has dragged a million human remnants into its heart. Bodies rot in soil, after all – and soil sits upon tectonic plates, plates eventually subduct into the fire under the Earth's crust, which convect endlessly through the mantle... and there within itself, the Earth eats everything they were. This is only fair, it reasons – coldly, with an anger that still shudders up from the depths to crack the world's skin and touch off Season after Season.</blockquote><br /><br />In every other human mythology it's understood that life comes from the earth and returns to earth and this is a good thing! Only in the twisted, miserable world of The Stillness is this not a natural part of the cycle, but an act of bitter revenge. It's also rather peculiar because presumably people were burying their dead long before the drilling started, which confusingly suggests that Earth was hating humanity long before they gave him a reason to – or perhaps somehow in the past he absorbed the dead but, like, not in a mean way? I don't know.<br /><br />Of course, Father Earth was just acting like every other character in the book. Alabaster began by tolerating the Fulcrum right up until he ripped the Earth in half. Essun kept her secrets in Tirimo until she murdered them all. Nobody in The Stillness seemed to understand the concept of attempting negotiation before waging war, attempting reform before launching revolution, attempting reconciliation rather than genocide, or ever, ever giving anyone else the benefit of the doubt.<br /><br />Which brings us to the Stone Eaters. It was pretty cool to discover that the stone eaters were created as a living bridge between the humans of the great civilisation of Syl Anagist and the powers of orogeny that the Sylanagistines were trying to control.<br />They were created in the image of The Niess, a tribe of people who were objectively better at using magic than the Sylanagistines, and were subsequently destroyed and dissected by the Sylanagistines in search of the biological cause of their superiority (of course, this is The Stillness, so apparently nobody thought to ask them first, or observe. Nope, it's all genocide, all the time, here on the Stillness).<br />So the Sylanagistines have created the Stone Eaters and actually given them the power that the Niess were only rumoured to have. Despite being their creators, the Sylanagistines were totally unaware of the real extent of the power of the Stone Eaters. This lead to an narrative of oppression that we're all familiar with from books 1 and 2. The Stone Eaters accepted their role until they were taken on a field trip of the city and learnt that the whole city was powered by the corpses of the Niess:<br /><br /><blockquote>Some of them we can see breathing, though the motion is so very slow. Many wear tattered rags for clothes, dry-rotted with years; a few are naked. Their hair and nails have not grown, and their bodies have not produced waste that we can see. Nor can they feel pain, I sense instinctively; this, at least, is a kindness. That is because the sinklines take all the magic of life from them save the bare trickle needed to keep them alive. Keeping them alive keeps them generating more.</blockquote><br /><br />I don't mean to be unfeeling, but this is a perpetual motion machine! If you put in a bare trickle of magic and yet they put out more magic than you put in, then you have an unlimited supply of energy and there's no need to drill to the core of the earth! Problem solved!<br />One might say that even if they Sylanagistines realised that they have <i>an actual source of unlimited energy!</i> it'd still wrong to use human beings as batteries. As a metaphor for slavery or imperialism, it's obvious as an anvil, but as something that we're supposed to accept as literally true within the world, it's a little hard to swallow. Hoa wondered about their scientist guide: <i>'It is as if he does not see what we're seeing. As if these stored, componentized lives mean nothing to him.'</i> It is indeed exactly like that! I had to re-read it to check that the bodies were literally there and not something only visible to the Stone Eaters. Why didn't they ask him about it? Why didn't they try to get him to acknowledge the vast corpse field or justify it? Why did people never, ever speak to each other like normal human beings in this book? No instead, they accepted it silently and began to plot - what else – mass-murder of the the Sylanagistines.<br />This is so frustrating for the reader. The Sylanagistines perpetrated some of the worst evil. And yet, in every other respect they're depicted as normal people. This is a disconnect that can only be bridged by letting them speak out and show how normal people rationalise and excuse the wrong that they do.<br />Likewise, it's not clear how these scientists controled and punished the Stone Eaters. There are references to it like: <i>'I understand precisely why Kelenli has spoken in this dismissive tone, and why she hasn't bothered to say farewell before leaving. It's no more than any of us do, when we must watch or sess another of our network punished, we pretend not to care.'</i> but without every saying what the punishments are!<br />I can't help but feel in the end that we were never told what the punishments are so that we never got to form our own judgements. Likewise, we never heard any Sylanagistine explanation for their actions, just in case we agreed with them! Much easier not to ask and just take Hoa's word for it, staying safely in his perspective.<br />And although we've seen enough horror to last a lifetime, what Hoa reacted to most is petty disrespect:<br /><br /><blockquote>They keep such lax security on us. […] Some of the sensors monitor our magic usage – and none of them, not one, can measure even a tenth of what we really do. I would be insulted if I had not just been shown how important it is to them that we be lesser. Lesser creatures don't need better monitoring, do they? Creations of Sylanagistine magestry cannot possibly have abilities that surpass it. Unthinkable! Ridiculous! Don't be foolish.<br />Fine. I am insulted. And I no longer have the patience for Stahnyn's polite patronization.</blockquote><br /><br />There is more rage about workplace patronization than there was about the field of corpses. And it's so woefully misplaced. Hoa assumed that they want him to be lesser, instead of realising that they might've genuinely believe it because he'd been making an effort to act that way: they didn't respect his power because he'd been carefully hiding it from them! And of course, it never occurred to him to gain their respect by showing his power or trying to initiate a conversation. No, he wanted a tighter prison and closer surveillance as proof of their respect!<br /><br />Hoa wasn't the only character with tragically misplaced priorities. Nassun, when deliberating whether to fix the world or destroy it, remembered the day when she came home to find that her father had murdered her brother. For three paragraphs she remembered in hideous detail her baby brother's broken, bleeding body. She remembered what a sweet boy he was, and how she made him laugh and she finally concluded: '<i>I wouldn't fix it, Schaffa. I wouldn't, I'm sorry, I don't want to fix it. I want to kill everybody that hates me!'</i><br />I was genuinely shocked at this. What kind of hideous narcissist is so self-centred that she remembers a murdered child and rages that they world hates <i>her</i> instead of <i>him</i>? Or even us! She could have wept for her brother, or for orogenes everywhere, but instead she wept only for herself.<br />Minor slips of the tongue that reveal the unpleasant nature of the characters are littered through out the book. At one point Essun gave a friend a compliment and thought: <i>'It's an olive branch. Or maybe just flattery. She doesn't fall for it.</i> People don't <i>fall</i> for olive branches, they <i>accept</i> them. Only a cynic who was trying trick her friend would think of it as <i>falling</i>.<br />Ok, ok, so I realise that I'm nitpicking now, but I came to find these characters so repellent that everything they said seemed to be poisonous. I felt as though this book were a world seen in a mirror that made everything beautiful seem ugly, and everything good seem sordid. It was just too bleak.<br /><br />Not only did I start nit-picking everything the characters said, but I loathed them so much that I diverted my attention to nitpicking the worldbuilding. After waiting for 3 books to wrap up, I can safely say that I don't quite believe the status of the orogenes in this world. They are hated and reviled everywhere, and it's revealed that this is because the Guardians, under the direction of Father Earth, have been spreading the hatred. But the orogenes have proven themselves far too useful. Castrima and Moev are the two of the more successful comms that we see in the book, and they both succeed due to the acceptance of orogenes. Book 3 is even filled with flavour-text about orogenes doing great things and saving towns. It seems like a much more stable equilibrium would be for comms to agree to harbor the orogenes and hide them from the Guardians, and in return the orogenes only use their power lawfully, if they try to hurt anyone with it then they get betrayed to the Guardians.<br /><br />If the stone-eaters could transport people through the earth in large numbers at vast speeds then why on Earth did the people of Castrima have to make that tedious forced march to Rennanis? Hoa could have ferried them all much faster and then they could've got on with the plot.<br /><br />I was holding out for the Season to be explained, but it never was. I know this is fantasy and not SF, but at the same time, I'm pretty sure that if there were a vent in the Earth the size of a continent, spewing so much detritus that it rained ash for two years, then the consequences would be a lot worse than just very ashy and a bit cold. When Mount Tambora erupted in 1815 then 1816 was known as 'The Year without Summer' or 'eighteen-hundred and froze to death'.<br /><br />OK, I'm becoming as petty as the characters in this book, banging on about minor discrepancies. Suffice to say that I'm glad that I read this series, although I've slated it. It had so much promise, so much inventiveness, so many moments of great writing and psychological insight, I only wish that they'd been stitched together better and that there had been somebody to like or something to alleviate the grim monotony of the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15845339728116432619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Philip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30863291-philip"><img alt="Philip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527983001p2/30863291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1834730725?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip" href="/user/show/30863291-philip">Philip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=top-5-2017">top-5-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=hugo-2018">hugo-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=hugo">hugo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=dying-earth">dying-earth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=author-woman">author-woman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=2017-releases">2017-releases</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=author-of-color">author-of-color</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30863291-philip?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1834730725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9387853890681245485">5ish stars. <br/><br/>This is an incredible series and a huge accomplishment for its author, N.K. Jemisin. It has every element of high quality fiction and, particularly, everything great about SFF. It's decidedly new age, but in a way that I don't doubt will stand the test of time. It's innovative in the way that Ursula K. Le Guin's work was innovative in the '60s and '70s (and continues to be influential today) and, maybe I'm foolish for saying this too soon, but I think it has the same potential to rea</span>
  <span id="freeText9387853890681245485" style="display:none">5ish stars. <br /><br />This is an incredible series and a huge accomplishment for its author, N.K. Jemisin. It has every element of high quality fiction and, particularly, everything great about SFF. It's decidedly new age, but in a way that I don't doubt will stand the test of time. It's innovative in the way that Ursula K. Le Guin's work was innovative in the '60s and '70s (and continues to be influential today) and, maybe I'm foolish for saying this too soon, but I think it has the same potential to reach classic status. <br /><br />Admittedly, the first book in the series, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19161852.The_Fifth_Season__The_Broken_Earth___1_" title="The Fifth Season (The Broken Earth, #1) by N.K. Jemisin" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Season</a>, is my favorite and I actually only rated that 4.5, but the series as a whole is greater than the sum of its individual parts. In this book specifically I found the first half to be slow, but once each of the separate storylines coalesced and the big picture came into view, the payoff was worth the journey. The ending wasn't mind-blowingly pulse-pounding but it was fitting and real and it felt <i>right</i>. It made me feel good.<br /><br />I respect Jemisin's talent immensely. Her prose is fantastic. She's not afraid to make her characters prickly, even unlikable at times and I love them all the more for it. Even the side characters who only make brief appearances are ones who I liked and would love to learn more about. The real greatness of this series is the world Jemisin has created- not only the "magic system," the blend of fantasy and sci-fi, or the diverse factions of characters, but how the nature of the world is primed to provide such deep commentary on so many grand, topical ideas. The way Jemisin addresses those ideas- duty, exploitation, love, humanity, morality, acceptance, xenophobia, motherhood, justice, mercy- is powerful and soulful and she doesn't take any easy ways out. She allows things to play out how they really do play out in human existence, not necessarily the ways we want them or expect them to in the literature we read. <br /><br />Congratulations to Jemisin for this achievement and congrats to me for getting to experience it!<br /><br /><a href="https://mrphilipslibrary.wordpress.com/2017/09/25/the-stone-sky-the-broken-earth-3-by-n-k-jemisin/" rel="nofollow noopener">Posted in Mr. Philip's Library</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9387853890681245485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1922810544" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1922810544" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41788446-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475256742p2/41788446.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1922810544?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/41788446-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41788446-hannah?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41788446-hannah?shelf=loved-it">loved-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1922810544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17814914371958671080">And this is how you end a trilogy. <br/><br/>This book was quite possibly (/definitely) my most anticipated book of the year; N. K. Jemisin has yet to dissappoint me and I just love love love her brand of fantasy. I love how intricate and well thought out her worlds are and how political they are at their core while she still never ever sacrifices her story to make a point. The final installment made me appreciate the overall brilliant work she has done in creating this cruel, wonderful, amazing world eve</span>
  <span id="freeText17814914371958671080" style="display:none">And this is how you end a trilogy. <br /><br />This book was quite possibly (/definitely) my most anticipated book of the year; N. K. Jemisin has yet to dissappoint me and I just love love love her brand of fantasy. I love how intricate and well thought out her worlds are and how political they are at their core while she still never ever sacrifices her story to make a point. The final installment made me appreciate the overall brilliant work she has done in creating this cruel, wonderful, amazing world even more. <br /><br />This world and its social structure makes so much sense and feels so real that it made me sad. It is perfectly structured to mirror our own world in miserable ways. I adore this political core and its relevance (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and its ultimate optimism  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). I adore the originality of the stone eaters (and their creation myth in particular) and how their interactions are always just a little bit off to never let the reader forget that they are <i>different</i>.<br /><br />But even more than the world building I adore the characters. They are what makes this book a true favourite for me: Essun and Nassun are such vividly imagined, flawed, wonderful creations and I adore how their actions and reactions mirror each other while they are still separate and complete characters in their own right. I love how this, at its core, is a story of family, blood and found, about how violence breeds violence, how mistakes can be repeated, how decisions shape our lives.<br /><br />On thing I realized upon finishing this book is how much I appreciate how N. K. Jemisin frames her stories; I love how the framing makes sense and its originality, and here I especially adore it. The framing device used fits perfectly to the world she has created here and to the way her story unfolds. <br /><br />So yes, brilliant way to end a brilliant trilogy. I cannot recommend the series enough. I am in love, still. (And heartbroken.)<br /><br />First sentences: "Time grows short, my love. Let's end with the beginning of the world, shall we? Yes. We shall."</span>
  <a data-text-id="17814914371958671080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer8375519148837744202">Finished this last night and I felt kind of unsure about it. I really enjoyed the whole series and I couldn't put the books down until I finished so the story telling was really good. Like I said through out I didn't really like any of the parts that were in second person POV and it felt less strong to have that in there, I think it only detracted from the books. Otherwise though I really enjoyed the books, though I'm not sure how I feel about the ending. I really liked the world building and th</span>
  <span id="freeText8375519148837744202" style="display:none">Finished this last night and I felt kind of unsure about it. I really enjoyed the whole series and I couldn't put the books down until I finished so the story telling was really good. Like I said through out I didn't really like any of the parts that were in second person POV and it felt less strong to have that in there, I think it only detracted from the books. Otherwise though I really enjoyed the books, though I'm not sure how I feel about the ending. I really liked the world building and the character development. I think the characters were all complex and multifaceted which is always a plus. I just think I always end up not enjoying endings regardless of whether or not theyre well written or built up to well. I think I only like endings that are sad and painful and this wasn't really like that. I wouldn't say it was even a bad ending per se. I don't think I liked this as much as the second book either and it did feel weaker than it. I think maybe it's something about series that I just like the books in the middle way better usually and the books at the end just feeling lacking in some way comparatively. The series was enjoyable though, would definitely recommend it. </span>

<div id="review_3581380906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3581380906" />
    <a title="Elle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70046142-elle"><img alt="Elle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548160841p2/70046142.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3581380906?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elle" href="/user/show/70046142-elle">Elle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3581380906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1252422996360788748">Well here we are, at the end of maybe the most immense series I’ve ever read. These books are nothing short of extraordinary and I will repeat what I said in my previous reviews: <b>they are a <u>must read</u> for sci-fi and fantasy fans</b>.<br/><br/><i>The Broken Earth</i> series is the perfect blend of science-fiction and fantasy, with some dystopia thrown in for good measure. Since this the the final book in the trilogy, I’m not goin to put any specific book spoilers in this review, and instead talk about the series in it</span>
  <span id="freeText1252422996360788748" style="display:none">Well here we are, at the end of maybe the most immense series I’ve ever read. These books are nothing short of extraordinary and I will repeat what I said in my previous reviews: <b>they are a <u>must read</u> for sci-fi and fantasy fans</b>.<br><br><i>The Broken Earth</i> series is the perfect blend of science-fiction and fantasy, with some dystopia thrown in for good measure. Since this the the final book in the trilogy, I’m not goin to put any specific book spoilers in this review, and instead talk about the series in its entirety.<br><br>At the center of these books is a story about a changing earth, which has both sharp differences and eerie similarities to our own. The Stillness, as it’s called, is subjected to a fifth season every indeterminate number of years, which unleashes cataclysmic levels of geological devastation onto the dispersed populations. People have organized themselves into Comms, as well as by castes, primarily as means of survival. Due to the unpredictable nature of these seasons, humanity’s growth outside of fulfilling basic needs has been stunted for thousands of years.<br><br>Among the humans exist a divergent set of people known as orogenes, who have varying levels of power over the earth itself. If someone is discovered to be an orogene, best case scenario they are sent to a training facility known as the Fulcrum to teach them to use their powers and contribute to society. Worst case they are killed by their communities before the Fulcrum’s Guardians are able to retrieve them.<br><br><i>“When we say that ‘the world has ended,’ remember—it is usually a lie. The planet is just fine.”</i><br><br>The world of <i>Ths Broken Earth</i> series is the way it is primarily because of human actions. The books take a harsh but necessary look at climate change and the human obstacles in the way of correcting previous environmental missteps. A huge stress by N.K. Jemisin is put on the fact that the Earth does not need life in order to exist, but we—life—need to preserve the earth if we plan on surviving ourselves. <br><br>But it’s the human elements of this series that are the real heart and soul of these books. Through the races of people on the page, Jemisin creates a stark comparison to how our own human history has subjugated and conquered other groups of people. Dehumanization is at the root of all of it, and is a necessary step in stripping people of their rights, livelihood and autonomy. And the generation trauma will be felt years and years beyond the initial offense, especially if left untreated and unacknowledged. History is written by the winners, and it must be thoroughly interrogated if you’re looking for anything that gives even a passing resemblance to the truth. <br><br><i>“Conquerors live in dread of the day when they are shown to be, not superior, but simply lucky.”</i><br><br>On a more personal level, Jemisin’s characters are filled to the brim with raw and often very painful emotions. They’re grieving and they’re despondent and they’re angry. There are moments of hope and levity, but this is a series that will make you <b>feel things</b>, so be prepared. So much of that hurt is rooted in love. The love of a mother to her children, the love between a child and surrogate parent. The love of a community and of humanity. Even when they all seem to let you down or fail you, that love is still present in the way you decide to keep living for them anyway. The mother/daughter relationship in particular between Nassun and Essun was so wrought and complicated it was hard for me not to start thinking about my relationship with my own mother. These books will really sink their claws into you.<br><br><i>“I think...that if you love someone, you don’t get to choose how they love you back.”</i><br><br>A brief note one complaint I keep hearing—I don’t really understand the people who are bothered by part of the books being written in second person. Maybe they’re just so used to first or third that second just throws them off completely? There’s a reason these portions are told in this way, one that’s relevant to the plot. And even if it was just a rhetorical or stylistic choice, that’s the prerogative of the author, in my opinion. It just feels like a lot of backseat writing to me, and if you get anything from reading this series, it’s that every choice N.K. Jemisin makes is deliberate, considered and probably brilliant.<br><br>This isn’t a quick or easy read. Some of the lore is very dense and you’re going to have to deal with unfamiliar terminology and names. But the examination of a complicated humanity, the kind of love that causes pain and what we owe one another &amp; the world we all inhabit is totally worth that commitment for me.<br><br><br><b>
  <i>“Don’t be patient. Don’t ever be. This is the way a new world begins.”</i>
</b><br><br><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1601901976ra/30196414.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Me after finishing the <i>Broken Earth</i> trilogy, contemplating what life’s existence even means.<i></i></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="1252422996360788748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2347790824442663375"><blockquote><b>*Spoilers*<br/><br/>You know there's something wrong when a major character dies yet it leaves you unmoved.</b> <br/><br/>Having cruised through the preceding serials, I was underwhelmed by this conclusion. <br/><br/>Not helping was the Info-dumping, likewise the glaring plot hole of the Onyx and Garnet Obelisk (read book one again to <u>sess</u> the inconsistency). <br/><br/>Additionally, the author's attempts at bringing some social issues to the fore was heavy handed, which is a shame really since the reader already caught on to these nuances</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2347790824442663375" style="display:none"><blockquote><b>*Spoilers*<br /><br />You know there's something wrong when a major character dies yet it leaves you unmoved.</b> <br /><br />Having cruised through the preceding serials, I was underwhelmed by this conclusion. <br /><br />Not helping was the Info-dumping, likewise the glaring plot hole of the Onyx and Garnet Obelisk (read book one again to <u>sess</u> the inconsistency). <br /><br />Additionally, the author's attempts at bringing some social issues to the fore was heavy handed, which is a shame really since the reader already caught on to these nuances right from the start of the series. <br /><br /><b>Overall, I didn't dislike how the story ended, I just didn't love it as much as the first two.</b></blockquote></span>
